Requirements for procedure trials, if different to the defaults shall only be provided if agreed with the
Overseeing Organisation.
NOTE 1: The defaults for the requirements for procedure trials are as specified in CC 486 [Ref 67.N].
NOTE 2: The procedure trials are to validate that the works can be executed with the proposed
supervision, labour, and equipment.
The requirement for provision of 'A' and 'B' samples may be amended where it is agreed with the
Overseeing Organisation that the extent of the maintenance painting is minor enough to not require the
'A' and 'B' samples for contract compliance testing.
E2.4
Verification of corrosion protection execution
E2.4.1
Verification of corrosion protection products through contract compliance testing
Verification of the compliance of paint supplied for corrosion protection shall be through contract
compliance testing of 'A' samples.
The requirements for provision of 'A' samples for contract compliance testing, if different to the defaults
shall be provided.
NOTE: The defaults for the requirements for provision of 'A' samples for contract compliance testing
are specified in Section 3 of CC 486 [Ref 67.N] .
The requirements for provision of 'A' samples for contract compliance testing may differ where
alternative arrangements have been agreed with the Overseeing Organisation for:
1)
testing frequency;
2)
packaging and dispatch times;
3)
provision and verification of receipt of delivery;
4)
collection from the address of the Overseeing Organisation's Testing Authority; or
5)
labelling of tins of paint.
'A' samples may not be required where the quantity of paint required to provide an 'A' sample is less
than the quantity of paint required to perform the painting work.
NOTE: Additional guidance on 'A' Samples is given in CG 303 [Ref 68.N].
The following information about the Overseeing Organisation's testing authority shall be provided:
1)
name;
2)
postal address; and
3)
email address.
NOTE The contact details of the Overseeing Organisation's testing authority are available from the
Overseeing Organisation's paint specialist.
E2.4.2
Verification of corrosion protection execution through testing
The paint testing requirements should provide confidence in the quality of the maintenance painting
execution, appropriate for the environment, complexity, and accessibility of the maintenance painting
scheme.
The frequencies of the following tests may be changed from the defaults:
1)
temperature and humidity testing;
2)
pin-holing and porosity;
3)
wet film thickness of paint; and
4)
paint adhesion.
331
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 333 of 417
======================================================================
CD 350 Version 1.0.0
Appendix E. Design outputs for protection of steelwork ag...
NOTE: The default work specific requirements for frequency of tests for protection of steelwork against
corrosion can be found in CC 486 [Ref 67.N].
Where dew point, temperature, or humidity are expected to be close to the allowable limits, the
frequency of temperature and humidity testing shall be increased to once before each shift and every 5
minutes thereafter.
NOTE: Dew point, temperature, or humidity are likely to be close to the allowable limits where Moisture
Cured, Solvent Free, or Single Pack paint products are used in coastal areas or near water courses.
Where inspection standards call for a defined light intensity to carry out a test, the required light
intensity shall be provided.
Where there is an elevated risk of pin-holing and/or porosity the frequency of testing of paint coats for
pin-holing and porosity may be increased to once for each area, covering a minimum of 20% of that
area.
NOTE: The risk of pin-holing and/or porosity is elevated in poor weather or where there is Difficult
Access.
The frequency of testing of the wet film thickness of paint shall be increased where there is an elevated
risk of the required dry film thickness not being achieved.
NOTE: Paint applied to components with complex geometry is at elevated risk of the required dry film
thickness not being achieved.
Where components are of a complex nature the frequency of paint adhesion testing may be increased.
Where inspection standards call for a defined light intensity to carry out a test, the required light
intensity shall be provided.
E2.4.3
Verification of corrosion protection execution through assessment by a paint inspector
The requirements for the paint inspector should be selected to provide confidence in the quality of the
painting execution, appropriate for the scale and complexity of the painting scheme.
The default requirements for paint inspectors shall only be amended if agreed with the Overseeing
Organisation.
Alternative paint inspector qualifications may be provided where they can be demonstrated to be
equivalent to the defaults listed.
NOTE: The default work specific requirements for paint inspectors for protection of steelwork against
corrosion can be found in CC 486 [Ref 67.N].
Relaxations to the requirement for the paint inspector to be from an independent organisation, such as
being from the same organisation or a subsidiary, should be commensurate with the scale of the
painting scheme.

Appendix F. Design outputs for masonry
Appendix F. Design outputs for masonry
F1
Design outputs for masonry
The outputs from the design of masonry shall include all details for the following:
1)
the location, setting out, and geometry of the masonry structure or feature;
2)
the masonry units, including material requirements, geometry (dimensions, shape, and features),
tolerances, and dressing;
3)
the masonry mortar, including requirements for constituent materials;
4)
the requirements for reinforcement and other metal ancillary components, including wall ties,
anchorages, and dowels;
5)
the layout and finish such as bond, coursing, jointing, and pointing;
6)
the accommodation of movement;
7)
the damp proof courses (DPC), waterproofing and drainage;
8)
any specific aesthetic requirements for the masonry, including the need for trial panels and any
consultation or approvals; and
9)
work specific constraints on execution.
Unreinforced masonry arch bridges shall be designed in accordance with CD 376 [Ref 108.N].
F1.1
Masonry reference
Each area of masonry or group of masonry structures or features with similar characteristics should be
assigned a unique reference to enable cross referencing.
Where a structure has masonry with different characteristics, it should be subdivided to allow for
variances in characteristics such as type of masonry unit and type of mortar.
NOTE: An example of different characteristics of masonry is where a brick wall has a stone coping.
This should be divided into two areas with unique masonry references for each.
Where a group of structures or features, such as retaining walls or chambers, have similar masonry
characteristics, these may be assigned a single unique masonry reference.
The structure name, structure reference, and structure location shall be consistent with information on
the Overseeing Organisation's asset management database.
F2
Specific design outputs for masonry
The specific design outputs for masonry shall be detailed on drawings and models including the
following:
1)
demarcation of each area of masonry with the masonry reference;
2)
the layout of the masonry, including setting out information, critical dimensions, bond, coursing,
and special units;
3)
details of reinforcement and other metal ancillary components, including wall ties, anchorages,
and dowels;
4)
details of the accommodation of movement; and
5)
details of damp proof courses (DPC), waterproofing and drainage.
For each unique masonry reference, the following shall be provided:
1)
a description of the area of masonry; and
2)
references to identify the drawing/model(s) that show the area of masonry.

Appendix F. Design outputs for masonry
F2.1
Waterproofing for masonry
Details of waterproofing of masonry shall include the following:
1)
waterproofing requirements, such as waterproof compound or sheeting specifications, extent and
protection; and
2)
references to identify the drawing/model(s) that show the required waterproofing.
When masonry waterproofing testing is undertaken, the following shall be provided:
1)
masonry reference, to identify the area of masonry to which the test applies;
2)
waterproofing characteristic to be verified;
3)
description of the testing, including references to testing standards where applicable;
4)
acceptance criteria;
5)
frequency of testing;
6)
documentation requirements; and
7)
any additional requirements.
F2.2
Aesthetics for masonry
Where aesthetic requirements for masonry are required, they shall be provided through the design
outputs for masonry Clause F1 8) details of the aesthetic requirements; aesthetic approval
requirements; and documentation requirements to provide evidence that the aesthetic requirements
have been complied with.
Structure specific aesthetic requirements shall be in accordance with the structure-specific Aesthetic
Appraisal Document, in accordance with CD 351 [Ref 102.N].
Where a trial panel is to be produced for aesthetic verification, the details shall include the following:
1)
masonry reference, to identify the area of masonry to which the test applies;
2)
whether a trial panel is required;
3)
trial panel requirements, to define the verification requirements to demonstrate that the aesthetic
requirements have been complied with;
4)
trial panel personnel requirements, to define any requirements for personnel relating to the trial
panel for masonry;
5)
documentation requirements; and
6)
any additional requirements.
NOTE: Trial panels may be used where:
1)
there are specific aesthetic requirements, such as in heritage areas; or
2)
complex construction techniques are required.
Note: Further details on trial panels for clay bricks is given in PAS 70 [Ref 55.I] and for natural stone in
BS 8298-2 [Ref 31.I].
F2.3
Masonry mortar
For masonry mortar, the characteristics listed in Table F.1 shall be provided.

Design output /
reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice or
notes for design outputs
Type of binder
Either: cement or lime
Note: Lime mortars have good
working qualities but develop strength
very slowly and are not widely used.
They are more commonly used for fine
architectural work, historic restoration,
and repointing of historic work.
Note: Lime for use as a binder
(typically hydraulic lime) should not be
confused with hydrated lime, which is
a common additive to cement-based
mortars to improve workability and
other properties.
Concept
Either: designed masonry mortar or
prescribed masonry mortar
Mode of
manufacture
Selected from: factory-made masonry
mortar, semi-finished factory made
masonry mortar, and site made
masonry mortar
Multiple options may be selected.
Note: Provision of a specific mode of
manufacture is not required.

set of three specimens for each type of mortar designation used on each structure, the requirements
should be provided.

F2.4
Masonry
The type of masonry shall be provided from one of: brick, concrete block, or stone.
Requirements for verification of the performance of masonry through work specific testing shall be
provided though the following for each test required:
1)
unique test reference, to identify the test required;
2)
masonry reference, to identify the area of masonry to which the test applies;
3)
masonry performance characteristic to be verified;
4)
description of test, including references to testing standards;
5)
acceptance criteria;
6)
frequency of testing;
7)
requirements for trial panels or samples;
8)
documentation requirements; and
9)
additional requirements.
F2.4.1
Reuse of site-won masonry
The design outputs shall identify where site-won masonry is available, and whether reuse is required
(i.e. site-won masonry needs to be reused), permitted (i.e. site-won masonry may be used) or not
permitted (i.e. site-won material is not for reuse).
Where reuse of site won masonry is required or permitted, cleaning or dressing, shall be undertaken in
accordance with LA 110 [Ref 55.N].
Where the reuse of site-won masonry is required or permitted, the following requirements for the
verification of site-won masonry units through the testing shall be provided:
1)
masonry unit characteristic to be verified;
2)
description of test, including references to testing standards where applicable;
3)
acceptance criteria;
4)
frequency of testing;
5)
documentation requirements; and
6)
additional requirements.

Bricks for masonry
The type of brick shall be selected from the following:
1)
Class A clay engineering bricks;
2)
Class B clay engineering bricks;
3)
clay masonry units; or
4)
calcium silicate masonry units.
Details of the locations where masonry P units, as defined in BS EN 771-1 [Ref 80.N] , are permitted
should be provided.
Masonry P units should only be used where the masonry is shielded or protected from the elements.
Where clay masonry units, other than Class A and Class B clay engineering bricks, are required, the

1 or 5.3.1.1.
Note: Standard UK brick
dimensions are 215 x 102.5 x 65
mm.
Dimensional tolerances
Tolerances of the mean value
shall be specified as a category.
Tolerances on the range shall be
specified as a category and are
only applicable where regularity of
units is important e.g. thin layer
joints.
Tolerances on flatness of bed
faces and plane parallelism shall
be specified as a limit on
deviation.
Requirements for dimensional
tolerances shall be provided in
accordance with BS EN 771-1
[Ref 80.N]5.2.1.2 or 5.3.1.2.
Note: The highest tolerance and
range category is T2 R2 and is
rarely achievable, or necessary
with standard UK brick sizes. T2
R1 is used for facework, or T1 R1
where a more traditional, irregular
shaped brick is used.
Note: Tolerances on the range are
only applicable where regularity of
units is important e.g. thin layer
joints, which are rarely used in the
UK, or very fine architectural work.
Note: Tolerances on flatness of
bed faces and plane parallelism
are only applicable where clay
masonry units are to be used with
thin layer mortar, which is rarely
used in the UK.
Shape and features
(configuration)
No restriction
Requirements for shapes and
features shall be provided in
accordance with BS EN 771-1
[Ref 80.N]5.2.2 or 5.3.2.
Note: Special shaped bricks are
used to provide architectural
details such as angled corners,
coping and cappings, sills, and
plinths. Guidance is provided in
BS 4729 [Ref 22.I]and from
manufacturers.
Configuration group
category
Group 1, Group 2, Group 3,
Group 4, no requirement
The requirements for group
category shall be provided in
accordance with BS EN 1996-1-1
[Ref 40.N]3.1.1.
Note: UK bricks typically fall into
either Group 1 (usually moulded
bricks and solid units) or Group 2
(usually extruded units).

A value in units of %

intended for use in damp proof

shall be provided in accordance
with BS EN 771-1 [Ref 80.N]5.3.7.
Note: Water absorption is an
important factor in the structural
design of brickwork to BS EN

the flexural strength of masonry
panels.
Note: Bricks with high water
absorption are more prone to
staining.

coefficient value, μ

intended for use in external

for this characteristic as a design
output is not usually required.

freeze-thaw

against freeze-thaw shall be

F2' should be used for facework or
where there is a risk of saturation
with freezing, otherwise F1 should
be used. F0 should only be used
for indoor applications.

F2.4.3
Concrete blocks for masonry
Where concrete blocks are required, the type of concrete block shall be selected from the following:
aggregate concrete masonry units; or autoclaved aerated concrete (AAC) masonry units.
Where aggregate concrete blocks are required, the performance characteristics listed in Table F.11
shall be provided.
Note: Aggregate concrete blocks can be used as either 'common' blocks (i.e. functional units, where
appearance is not important), as 'paint grade' blocks, or as 'facing' blocks, which influences
performance characteristics such as dimensions and tolerances.

where coordination with brickwork
is required, or 390 x 190 mm
where coordination with metric
modules is required. Widths vary
as required.

subject to structural requirements,
requirements for shear bond
strength shall be provided in
accordance with BS EN 771-5
[Ref 81.N] 5.12.
Note: Provision of a specific value
for this characteristic as a design
output is not usually required.

strength
A value in units of N/mm
Where the masonry units are

subject to structural requirements,
requirements for flexural bond
strength shall be provided in
accordance with BS EN 771-5
[Ref 81.N] 5.13.
Note: Provision of a specific value
for this characteristic as a design

Reaction to fire
A1, A2, B, C, D, E, F, no
requirement
Where the masonry units are
intended for use in elements
subject to fire requirements, the
reaction to fire classification shall
be stated in accordance with BS
EN 771-5 [Ref 81.N] 5.11.
Note: Manufactured stone units
are typically A1 Class and this can
be specified in most instances.
Maximum water absorption
by capillarity
A value in units of g/(m2 s)
Where the masonry units are
intended for use in damp-proof
courses or in external elements
with exposed faces, requirements
for maximum water absorption
shall be provided in accordance
with BS EN 771-5 [Ref 81.N] 5.8.1
.
Note: Water absorption is an
important factor in the structural
design of brickwork to BS EN
1996 [Ref 41.N], which can affect
the flexural strength of masonry
panels.
Note: Units with high water
absorption are more prone to
staining.

F2.5
Reinforcement and metal ancillary components
F2.5.1
Reinforcement
Where masonry is reinforced, the details of the reinforcement shall be provided through drawings /
models and reinforcement schedules.
Where reinforcement for masonry is required, reference(s) to the drawings / models that detail the
reinforcement for masonry shall be provided.
Where wire fabric or plain bar laid between masonry is required, the stainless steel grade shall be
provided, selected from the following: 1.4301, 1.4436.
Note: Grade 1.4301 is permitted for most atmospheric and covered environments, grade 1.4436 is
permitted for marine and other corrosive environments.
F2.5.2
Metal ancillary components
Requirements for metal ancillary components for masonry shall be provided.
Reference(s) to the drawings / models that detail the metal ancillary components should be provided.

Appendix F. Design outputs for masonry
F2.6
Execution of masonry
F2.6.1
Brickwork and blockwork
Where masonry consists of brickwork or blockwork, the requirements for their execution shall be
provided for the following:
1)
bond type;
2)
coursing;
3)
jointing: locations and finish;
4)
pointing: locations and finish;
5)
reinforcement: laps and cover; and
6)
wetting before laying.
F2.6.2
Stonework
Where masonry consists of stonework, the layout of stonework and dimensions of stones shall be
provided in the drawings or models.
A stone's length shall not exceed three times its height.
The breadth on the bed of stonework shall not be less than 150 mm.
The breadth on the bed of stonework shall not be greater than three-quarters of the thickness of the
wall.
Where stonework is required, the jointing and pointing of stonework shall be provided, through the
following:
1)
jointing: locations and finish; and
2)
pointing: locations and finish.
Where ashlar or block-in-course stonework are required, the requirements listed in Table F.15 shall be
provided.

Appendix G. Design outputs for miscellaneous materials (b...
Appendix G. Design outputs for miscellaneous materials (bedding mortar,
concrete for ancillary purposes, porous no fines concrete and
other materials used as permeable backing to earth retaining
structures, and foamed concrete for structures)
G1
Design outputs for miscellaneous materials
The design outputs associated with work specific requirements for construction with miscellaneous
materials shall be provided in accordance with this document.
Note: work-specific requirements (WSRs) for miscellaneous materials can be found in CC 495 [Ref
56.N] and CC 500 [Ref 22.N].
Drawings or models shall be produced to clearly show the location, dimensions, tolerances, setting out
and orientation of elements to be constructed using the miscellaneous materials covered by this
document.
Note: A unique reference can be assigned to each element or group of elements with similar
characteristics.
Referencing of elements should be linked with the associated structure name, structure reference or
asset identifier.
Structure names, structure references or other asset identifiers associated with elements of
miscellaneous materials should be consistent with the Overseeing Organisation's asset management
information system.
G2
Design outputs for high strength bedding mortar plinths
G2.1
Advice for applications for high strength bedding mortar
High strength bedding mortar plinths or other thin layers of high strength bedding mortar intended as
load transmitting platforms can be used for the following applications:
1)
structural bridge bearings;
2)
metal baseplates under VRS posts, metal gantries, metal mast structures and other metal
structural sections; and
3)
precast concrete structural elements intended for bridges or retaining wall applications.
G2.2
General requirements for outputs from design of bedding mortar plinths or other thin layers
The outputs from design of discrete bedding mortar plinths or other thin layers of high strength bedding
mortar shall include requirements for construction with miscellaneous materials given in sub-section G1
of this document and the following details:
1)
thickness of bedding mortar plinths;
2)
geometry of bedding mortar plinths;
3)
any reinforcement in plinths;
4)
the standard of surface finish for plinth sides or layer edges; and
5)
any other aesthetic requirements.
G2.3
High strength bedding mortar
Where there are scheme-specific requirements for the following aspects of high strength bedding
mortar, the characteristics listed in Table G.1 shall be provided.
1)
a designed mortar approach is used as an alternative to a proprietary product (with reference to
BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]) for a high strength cementitious bedding mortar;

Permitted thickness range of
proprietary bedding mortar
plinth.
(where different to the range of

Provide a range of thickness
permitted for the mortar plinth.
Provide values of minimum to
maximum thickness range in
mm.
Vertical loading from the
baseplate is dispersed to
supporting structural element
through the bedding mortar
plinth.
Notional lines of load dispersal
in the vertical direction within
the plinth material can be used
in the design of the plinth to
derive the plan dimensions of
the plinth.
Note: advice on load dispersal
can be found in BS EN 1991-2
[Ref 25.N].
Note: A range of mortar plinth
thickness between 10 mm and
30 mm is permitted according to
CC 495 [Ref 56.N].
Note: The permitted thickness
range is given for construction
of a robust plinth or for reliable
filling of the gap.
Note: The 30 mm maximum
thickness is given to minimise
creep under loading or
shrinkage of the mortar and limit
risk of structural damage of
elements above.
Vertical loading dispersal lines
can be allowed to occur outside
the plinth if the plinth is
reinforced.
Note: Where the mortar plinth is
thicker than 30 mm, horizontal
reinforcement can be provided
within the bedding mortar.
Note: Alternatively, to
accommodate larger vertical
gaps, a bedding mortar plinth
with the usual thickness range
can be used with a reinforced
concrete raised plinth.
NOTE: Use of a proprietary bedding mortar is assumed for construction of high strength bedding mortar
plinths according to CC 495 [Ref 56.N]. Plinth construction using a high strength designed cementitious
bedding mortar can be used as an alternative option.

Design requirements,
advice or notes for design
outputs
Minimum 28-day compressive
strength (of designed
cementitious bedding mortar
if not 50 MPa).
If a higher strength is needed,
provide the minimum compressive
strength of designed cementitious
bedding mortar when tested at 28
days.
Provide value in MPa.
If the design needs mortar
plinth strength to be greater
than 50 MPa divided by
material partial safety factors,
a higher strength of mortar
may be used.
Note: The 28-day minimum
compressive cube strength of
bedding mortar is 50 MPa
according to CC 495 [Ref
56.N].
Note: Products or materials
with strengths greater than 50
MPa can be more
temperature sensitive and
variable.

cement content, by mass of cement
per unit volume of designed
cementitious bedding mortar.
Provide value in kg/m3.
The minimum cement content
depends on the design
working life and exposure
conditions of plinth in service.
Note: refer to section 5 of this
document for further
information on design working
life of highway structures or
structural elements.
Note: refer to BS 8500-1 [Ref
12.N]for information on
exposure conditions of the
plinth, however unless the
plinth contains steel
reinforcement, the purpose of
using exposure conditions for
design of the mortar plinth is
to determine the required
mortar strength.

Maximum cement content.

per unit volume of designed

Provide value in kg/m3.
The maximum cement

with achieving the correct
strength in the required time
period.
Note: if the cement binder in
the mortar used has a low
proportion of cement
replacement material (e.g.
CEM I), using a high
maximum cement content can
increase the risk of early
thermal cracking occurring in
the bearing plinth.

cementitious bedding mortar
should not exceed 550 kg/m3.
Maximum effective
water/cement ratio.
Provide the maximum permitted
effective water/cement ratio in the
designed cementitious bedding
mortar.

12.N], BS 8500-2 [Ref
13.N]and BS EN 206 [Ref
11.N] for advice on maximum
permitted effective
water/cement ratio.

cementitious bedding mortar plinth
if not 2.8mm.

for maximum aggregate size
in table G.2

(reference BS EN 206 [Ref
11.N]).
Provide chloride content class for
the high strength designed
cementitious bedding mortar to be
used.
Permitted options limited to:
1) Cl 0.10
2) Cl 0.20
3) Cl 0.40
The chloride class chosen
shall be consistent with the
sum of chloride limits for
constituent materials
(aggregate, cement, water)
given in BS EN 206 [Ref
11.N].
Note: A high chloride content
permitted for the mortar (0.4%
by mass of cement or greater)
can cause damage to
protective coatings on metal
baseplates.

plinth. The notional lines of
load dispersal in the vertical
direction within the plinth
material is used in the design
of the plinth to derive the plan

designed so notional lines of
dispersal occur within the
plinth material, unless the
plinth is reinforced.
Note: A range of mortar plinth
thickness between 10 mm
and 30 mm is permitted
according to CC 495 [Ref
56.N].
The recommended thickness
range is given to ensure
reliable filling of the gap and
good quality plinth
construction, but is not too
great that creep under
permanent loading and
shrinkage could cause
structural damage to the
supported element.
If gaps to be filled between
surfaces are greater than 30
mm, horizontal reinforcement
can be provided in the
bedding mortar. Alternatively
a low reinforced concrete
plinth can be used in
conjunction with a bedding
mortar plinth.

using a convenient concrete mix method with the mass of coarse aggregate set to zero e.g. BRE BR

G3
Design outputs for concrete used for ancillary purposes
G3.1
Scope of concrete used for ancillary purposes
The requirements and design outputs given in this section shall apply to lower strength concrete used
for ancillary purposes including construction applications related to highway structures, and
construction applications for other highway assets as follows:
1)
fencing;
2)
environmental barriers;
3)
road restraint systems;
4)
drainage;
5)
earthworks;
6)
pavements;
7)
traffic signs;
8)
road lighting; and
9)
communications.
NOTE 1: Ancillary concretes have lower strength, contain less cement and have higher water cement
ratio compared with structural concretes and can provide less protection to any embedded steel
reinforcement.
Where technical approval is needed for design of concrete elements with a predominantly structural
function according to CG 300 [Ref 94.N], a designed concrete with minimum strength class C28/35
should be used instead of an ancillary concrete.
NOTE 2: Examples of structures or other assets where technical approval is needed for design of
reinforced structural concrete foundations include:
1)
traffic signs or signals;
2)
lighting columns;
3)
high mast lighting and CCTV masts;
4)
cantilever masts;
5)
other mast type structures; and
6)
'fence type' structures e.g. environmental barriers, visual screens, fencing.
NOTE 3: See requirements and advice in CD 354 [Ref 18.N]and concrete guidance in BS 8500-1 [Ref
12.N]

Ancillary concrete used in non-aggressive ground conditions
Where ancillary concrete is used in ground conditions classified as non-aggressive, table G.6 describes
the construction applications for which BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] designated concrete types are required
and other named construction applications for which BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] designated concrete types
are recommended.
NOTE 1: Non-aggressive ground conditions can be assumed as the aggressive chemical environment
for concrete (ACEC class) that requires concrete to have a design chemical class (DC class) of no
greater than DC-1 to resist chemical attack in accordance with BRE SD-1 [Ref 84.I]and BS 8500-1 [Ref
12.N]

G3.5
Grade enhancement of ancillary concrete used in ground conditions classified as aggressive
Where lower strength concrete for ancillary purposes is used in ground conditions classified as
'aggressive', designated concrete mixes required for construction applications or recommended for
other example applications described in Table G.6 of this document shall be enhanced to a more
durable grade to resist chemical attack for the intended working life of the concrete.
NOTE 1: Existing geotechnical data or outputs from new geotechnical survey can be used to identify
aggressive ground conditions in locations where ancillary concrete is used and hence if enhanced
concrete designations are needed.
NOTE 2: An enhancement of concrete grade for aggressive ground conditions in accordance with BRE
SD-1 [Ref 84.I] can be assumed for all design chemical classes except DC-1.
NOTE 3: Further details of construction applications listed in Table G.6 can be found in CC 120 [Ref
28.I], CC 207 [Ref 43.I], CC 401 [Ref 62.I], CC 481 [Ref 59.I], CC 500 [Ref 22.N], LC 127 [Ref 40.I] or
TC 131 [Ref 82.I].
NOTE 4: Grade enhancement can be achieved using a designated FND grade or higher grades of
designated GEN or RC concrete subject to restrictions on use described in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N].
NOTE 5: Intended working life for concrete located in aggressive ground can be found in Section 5 of
this document for concrete elements of highway structures, LC 127 [Ref 40.I] for fencing works, CD 377
[Ref 71.N] for legacy road restraint systems, CG 501 [Ref 17.N]for drainage, CD 120 [Ref 19.N]for
signs and CD 354 [Ref 18.N] for lighting and masts

Air-entrainment required
Identify whether air-entrainment of
ancillary concrete is needed for
defining the ancillary concrete
product requirements.
Permitted options limited to:
1) Yes
2) No
Saturated concrete of
strength less than C32/40 can
be damaged when ambient
temperature drops lower than
the freezing point of water,
particularly if the concrete is
used in locations exposed to
deicing salts. Entraining air
into the concrete can lower
the risk of extensive damage
by limiting the propagation of
micro-cracking.
See further advice from
Concrete@your fingertips
[Ref 24.I]

% and 6 % inclusive.

in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]and

Permeable backing material
(used in conjunction with
Class 7 selected cohesive
earthworks fill around
structures or chalk, class Dc
or Dm fill around structures in
accordance with CC 601 [Ref
23.N])
Provide the permeable backing
material type where the fill adjacent
to the structure is selected cohesive
material (Class 7A) or selected
conditioned pulverised-fuel ash
(Class 7B) or chalk in accordance
with CC 601 [Ref 23.N]).
Permitted options limited to:
1) options 1) to 3) above for class
6 backfill are not applicable for
class 7 fill
2) sand (where adjacent fill is class
7A, class 7B or chalk)
Sand may be used as a
permeable backing material
where the fill consists of any
of the following backfill types
classified in accordance with
CC 601 [Ref 23.N]- cohesive
material (Class 7A), selected
conditioned pulverised-fuel
ash (class 7B) or chalk.
Note: permeable backing
materials used in conjunction
with class 7 selected cohesive
earthworks fill for earth
retaining structures and
material performance
requirements can be found in
CC 500 [Ref 22.N].
Note: Class Dc chalk is often
regarded as a granular
material but can degrade to a
more cohesive soil releasing
fine particles, so all chalk
adjacent to a permeable
backing drainage layer is
assumed as cohesive.
Sand fine aggregate with
grading ranges of 0/2, 0/4 and
coarseness of CP or MP are
permitted according to CC
500 [Ref 22.N].
Note: The sand grading
prevents very fine particles
contained in the backfill from
entering and clogging up the
drainage layer.
Migration of fine particles into
a drainage layer can leave
voids in the fill and can result
in settlement of the ground.

G5
Design outputs for foamed concrete intended for infilling of structures
G5.1
Foamed concrete used for infilling structures and other assets
Foamed concrete for highway structures may be used for the following applications:
1)
Bulk infilling of redundant highway structures and including bridges, subways, culverts, large
diameter pipes (>0.9 m diameter) and services tunnels;
2)
Filling of gaps between an existing load carrying member and a new load carrying member for
the purpose of strengthening by load transfer;
3)
Filling of gaps between the ground and new load bearing structural element;
4)
Relining an existing asset for durability or hydraulic flow reasons;
5)
Replacement of normal weight fill by lighter weight fill above masonry arches, and adjacent to
masonry spandrels and approach retaining walls for the purpose of strengthening by load
reduction;
6)
Backfill behind retaining walls or bridge abutments or infill around bridge abutment frames to
reduce earth pressures; and
7)
Infilling to abandon other redundant assets including chambers, tunnel ventilation shafts, mine
shafts etc.
Note: Examples of gaps to be filled can include behind new lining of culverts or sewers, or behind
tunnel segments and tunnel grouting.
Note: This document does not cover foamed concrete used for the following applications:
1)
slope stability in embankment construction for geotechnical assets;
2)
low density mass fill or blinding for construction in poor/weak ground;
3)
abandoned/redundant drainage pipes;
4)
reinstatement of trenches for drainage or communications assets; nor
5)
pavement construction.
G5.2
Risk factors associated with installing foamed concrete
The following risks and hazards should be included in the design risk assessment for foamed concrete
during installation when the material is still fluid and post installation when the material has hardened
as follows:
394
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 396 of 417
======================================================================
CD 350 Version 1.0.0
Appendix G. Design outputs for miscellaneous materials (b...
1)
Foamed concrete flows readily and can infiltrate, and block nearby damaged drainage pipes or
unsealed ducting located within or immediately adjacent to the zone being infilled;
2)
Unguarded or exposed foamed concrete can present a risk of drowning until the foamed concrete
has achieved its final set; and
3)
Foamed concrete can contain aggregates that generate ignitable gases e.g. incinerator bottom
ash containing trace metals from fluid or hardened concrete. There can be a risk of explosion
when foamed concrete is used in confined spaces.
G5.3
General requirements for outputs from design of foamed concrete intended for infilling
structures and other assets
The outputs from design of foamed concrete for infilling of structures an other assets shall include the
requirements for construction with miscellaneous materials given in Section G1 of this document.
Foamed concrete mix proportions should be designed to yield a hardened concrete material with a
cube compressive strength between 4 N/mm2 and 10 N/mm2 inclusive when tested at age 7 days.
The proportions of constituent materials for foamed concrete should be designed during the
construction phase.
Where design of a foamed concrete mix is based on a proprietary foamed concrete product, strength
requirements for the mix should be confirmed by evidence obtained from previous development testing.

Appendix H. Design outputs for bridge bearings
Appendix H. Design outputs for bridge bearings
The outputs from the design of bridge bearings should include all details to define the following:
1)
the location, setting out, orientation and geometry of the existing and/or proposed bridge
bearings; and
2)
the type and/or model of the existing and/or proposed bridge bearings.
The performance requirements for the proposed bridge bearings should be provided in a bearing
schedule produced in accordance with Annex A of PD 6703 [Ref 90.N] for each bridge bearing, or
group of bridge bearings with similar characteristics.
Guidance on selection of performance characteristics for bridge bearings is given in PD 6703 [Ref
90.N].
Drawings or models should be produced showing the location, setting out and orientation of the the
bridge bearings.
Each bridge bearing, or group of bridge bearings with similar characteristics should be assigned a
unique reference to enable cross referencing.
The structure name, structure reference and structure location should be consistent with information on
the Overseeing Organisation's highways structures information management system.

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and notes for design
outputs for structural concrete
This Appendix gives design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for structural concrete
that are additional to those given, for specific design outputs, in the Tables of Appendix C.
I1
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for execution
management of structural concrete
I1.1
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for special testing requirements for
the inspection of execution for structural concrete
I1.1.1
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Non-destructive testing required',
'Requirements for non-destructive testing including frequency' and 'Special testing
specification documentation reference(s)'
Type, extent and frequency of non-destructive testing depend on the nature of the deficiency to be
looked at, the structural concrete element type (such as unreinforced, reinforced or prestressed
concrete) and its location and function within the works. Design requirements, advice and guidance on
non-destructive testing and the criteria to decide their type, extent and frequency are given in CS 464
[Ref 60.I], CBDG TG2 [Ref 52.I]and IMfHS [Ref 57.I].
For hardened concrete, non-destructive testing techniques that have been used successfully include:
1)
Gamma radiography. Gamma radiography has been used to test concrete up to 450 mm thick for
the presence of local voids in the concrete and the efficiency of the grouting of ducts in
post-tensioned concrete elements; the presence and location of embedded metal may also be
determined. When gamma radiography is specified for use, the design outputs shall require that
it is carried out in accordance with BS 1881-205 1986 [Ref 88.I]
2)
Ultrasonic testing and rebound hammer testing. If an ultrasonic apparatus or rebound hammer is
regularly used by trained personnel, and if continuously maintained individual charts are kept that
show the relation between the readings and the strength of cubes made from the same batch of
concrete for a large number of readings, such charts may be used to obtain approximate
indications of the strength of the concrete in a structure or element. When ultrasonic testing and
rebound hammer testing are specified for use, the design outputs shall require that they are
carried out in accordance with BS EN 13791 [Ref 7.I]and PD CEN/TR 17086 [Ref 45.I]. In cases
of suspected lack of compaction or low cube strengths, ultrasonic tests carried out on adjacent
suspect and acceptable sections of the structure may provide useful comparative data. When
rebound hammer testing is specified for use, the design outputs shall require that readings from
rebound hammer testing are not taken within 25 mm of the edge of the concrete members, and
that a distinction is made between readings taken on a trowelled face and those taken on a
moulded face. When rebound hammer testing is specified for use for precast units, the design
outputs shall require that the precast units are bedded firmly against the impact of the hammer
3)
Cover measurement testing. The position and cover of reinforcement, tendons and metal ducts
may be verified by cover measuring devices, as described in CBDG TG2 [Ref 52.I]. When the
use of electromagnetic cover measuring devices is specified, the design outputs shall require that
electromagnetic cover measurement is carried out in accordance with BS 1881-204 [Ref 89.I].
I1.1.2
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Destructive testing required',
'Requirements for destructive testing including frequency' and 'Special testing specification
documentation reference(s)'
Type, extent and frequency of destructive testing depend on the nature of the deficiency to be looked
at, the structural concrete element type (such as unreinforced, reinforced or prestressed concrete) and
its location and function within the works. Guidance on destructive testing and the criteria to decide
their type, extent and frequency is given in CBDG TG2 [Ref 52.I]and IMfHS [Ref 57.I].
For hardened concrete, a destructive testing technique that can be used when there are concerns
regarding the actual concrete strength is coring from the structure for the purpose of assessing the
401
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

======================================================================
CD 350 Version 1.0.0
Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
worst credible strength of the concrete in compression in accordance with CS 455 [Ref 93.I]. When
coring for evaluating the concrete compressive strength is specified, the design outputs shall require
that it is carried out in accordance with BS EN 13791 [Ref 7.I]and PD CEN/TR 17086 [Ref 45.I]. Cores
cut from a structural concrete element may also be specified with the purpose of investigating the
presence of voids in the compacted concrete.
When coring is specified, the design outputs shall either require that core cutting avoids existing
reinforcement, or provide any constraints on cutting existing reinforcement.
I1.1.3
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Dimensional accuracy testing required',
'Requirements for dimensional accuracy testing including frequency' and 'Special testing
specification documentation reference(s)'
Type, extent and frequency of dimensional accuracy testing depend on the nature of the geometrical or
positional dimension being looked at, the structural concrete element type (such as unreinforced,
reinforced or prestressed concrete) and its location and function within the works.
When the reason for specifying dimensional accuracy testing is due to concerns resulting from the
outcome of the inspection for execution or other tests, the extent and frequency of the specified
dimensional accuracy testing should be proportionate to the criticality of the consequence of any
non-conformance.
Whilst in the majority of cases the choice of the type of testing and method of measurement can be
done at construction stage, in some circumstances it may be needed to specify the method of
measurement of the dimensional accuracy. Examples include:
1)
Where the geometrical or positional dimension being looked at requires to be checked in a
specific way or sequence which is dependent on the design assumptions
2)
Where specific equipment is required, such as a special measuring frame for checking twist, bow,
squareness and flatness of precast units.
When dimensional accuracy testing is specified for checking the camber or upward deflection of precast
units due to prestress, the design outputs should require that the measurement is undertaken by either:
1)
Placing the precast unit on bearings at full span, with a central reference point provided level with
the bearings and measuring the upward deflection on the underside; or
2)
Using dial gauges or measurements from a thin wire stretched across the bearings and tensioned
enough to take out the sag.
I1.1.4
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Load testing required', 'Requirements for
load testing including frequency' and 'Special testing specification documentation reference(s)'
Type, extent and frequency of load testing depend on the nature of the deficiency being looked at, the
structural concrete element type (such as unreinforced, reinforced or prestressed concrete), its location
and function within the works and the structure type and articulation.
When load testing is specified, it shall be specified in accordance with the requirements below, where
deflection is defined as the maximum amount of movement under load of the element being tested
relative to a straight line connecting its points of support. The requirements below are intended to be
applicable for checking a structural element or the whole structure, but not for model testing when used
as a basis of design, development testing of prototype structures or testing to prove the adequacy of a
structure owing to change of use or loading. Design requirements and advice on load testing
specifically for assessment are provided in CS 463 [Ref 53.N].
When load testing is specified, the values of the theoretical deflection under load for the structure or
structural element to be load tested shall be defined in the design outputs, with due allowance made for
the possible effects of variation in temperature and humidity during the period of the test. When load
testing is specified for prestressed concrete due allowance should be made, in defining the specific
deflection limits representing the theoretical deflection under load, for the effect of prestress at the time
of testing being above its final value.

Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
When load testing for individual precast concrete units is specified, the design outputs shall require that
the loads are applied and removed incrementally and that testing and acceptance criteria are in
accordance with the following:
1)
Non-destructive load testing. The unit is supported at its designed points of support and is loaded
for 5 minutes with a load equivalent to the sum of the nominal self-weight plus 1.25 times the
nominal imposed load, and deflection and crack widths are measured; the acceptance criteria is
that the maximum deflection measured after application of the load remains within the theoretical
deflection under load defined in the design, and at no time during testing there are evident signs
of distress or faulty construction through visual observation or interpretation of deflection/crack
width measurement data. The recovery is measured 5 minutes after the removal of the applied
load, the load is reimposed for 5 minutes and the recovery measured 5 minutes after the removal
of the reimposed load; the acceptance criteria is that the percentage recovery measured after the
second loading is not less than the percentage recovery measured after the first loading, nor less
than 90% of the deflection recorded during the second loading.
2)
Destructive load testing. The unit is loaded to its ultimate design load while supported at its
designed points of support and the deflection is measured; the acceptance criteria is that the unit
does not fail at its ultimate design load within 15 minutes of the time when the test load starts,
and the deflection does not exceed one-fortieth of the span.
When load testing for very large precast concrete units, units not readily amenable to tests (such as
columns, the precast parts of composite beams and members designed for continuity or fixity) or
prestressed units is specified, the testing arrangements and allowable deflections shall be specified on
a case-by-case scenario.
When load testing for entire structures or part of structures is specified, the design outputs shall require
that the loads are applied and removed incrementally and that testing and acceptance criteria are in
accordance with the following:
1)
Age at test. The test is carried out as soon as possible after the expiry of 28 days from the time of
concrete placing, or after the expiry of the compressive strength compliance age when the
compressive strength compliance age is different than 28 days. When the test is for a reason
other than the quality of the concrete in the structure being in doubt, the test may be carried out
earlier provided that the concrete has already reached its specified characteristic strength
2)
Test loads. The test loads applied for deflection and serviceability checks are the nominal
self-weight and imposed loads. For ultimate limit state checks, the test load applied is equivalent
to the sum of the nominal self-weight plus 1.25 times the nominal imposed load and is maintained
for a period of 24 hours. If any of the final self-weight load is not in position on the structure,
compensating loads are added. During the test, struts and bracing strong enough to support the
whole load are placed in position leaving a gap under the members to be tested, and precautions
are taken to safeguard persons in the vicinity of the structure
3)
Measurements during the tests. Measurements of deflection and crack widths are taken, with
enough redundancy in the number of measurements, immediately after the application of the
load, at the end of the 24-hour loading period, immediately after removal of the load and after a
24-hour recovery period. Temperature and weather conditions are recorded during the tests
4)
Assessment of results. Acceptance criteria are as follows:
a)
The deflection measured immediately after application of the test load for deflection and
serviceability checks does not exceed the theoretical deflection under load defined in the
design, and at no time during testing there are evident signs of distress or faulty
construction through visual observation or interpretation of deflection/crack width
measurement data
b)
For an unreinforced or reinforced concrete structure or part of it, the percentage recovery
measured 24 hours after removal of the test load for ultimate limit state checks is at least
75% of the maximum deflection recorded during the load testing. If this is not met, the load
testing procedure is repeated and the acceptance criteria is that the percentage recovery
measured after the second load testing is at least 75% of the maximum deflection recorded
during the second load testing
403

c)
For a prestressed concrete structure or part of it, the percentage recovery measured 24
hours after removal of the test load for ultimate limit state checks is at least 85% of the
maximum deflection recorded during the load testing. If this is not met, the load testing
procedure is repeated and the acceptance criteria is that the percentage recovery
measured after the second load testing is at least 85% of the maximum deflection recorded
during the second load testing.
I2
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for steel
reinforcement for structural concrete
I2.1
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for hot rolled and cold worked
stainless steel bars for the reinforcement of structural concrete
I2.1.1
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Reason for hot rolled or cold worked
stainless steel bars requirement'
The use of correctly specified stainless steel with enhanced resistance to corrosion instead of carbon
steel can enhance durability by minimising the damage in those elements of highway structures most at
risk from chloride attack, and reduce maintenance and associated traffic disruption.
Although the unit cost of stainless steel bars, whose prices are volatile and can vary considerably for
different bar sizes, have historically varied between approximately four and ten times that of carbon
steel bars, for highway structures the cost of selective use of stainless steel reinforcement represents a
small percentage of the overall cost and the additional expenditure may be justified, in whole life terms,
in locations like parapet edge beams, splash zones of substructures adjacent to carriageways, bearing
shelves below movement joints, substructures in marine environments and superstructures subject to
spray.
Full substitution of carbon steel reinforcement with stainless steel reinforcement should be subject to a
whole life cost benefit analysis and may be advantageous for major components of new structures
where future repair and maintenance works would be very costly and disruptive to traffic, such as decks
of bridges carrying heavily trafficked roads over busy railway lines, exposed piers and columns in
centre reserves and deck slabs with difficult maintenance access because of traffic levels.
Stainless steel reinforcement should not be specified for structures and components that are remote
from the carriageway, are not exposed to high concentration of chlorides or will not need traffic
management for maintenance, and this category can include foundations, piles, buried structures,
elements protected by effective waterproofing and superstructure elements of accommodation bridges
and footbridges over the carriageway.
The use of stainless steel may also be justified for the repair and replacement of elements that have
suffered widespread chloride-induced corrosion and for modification or strengthening works. In these
cases, the stainless steel reinforcement should not be lapped onto bars where corrosion has already
started or where chloride levels or half cell readings indicate corrosion may be imminent, as there is a
risk that corrosion resistance may be impaired and the existing carbon steel may already be active and
vulnerable to bi-metallic corrosion with the stainless steel - this risk does not apply, however, to new
construction where both carbon and stainless steel are cast in new concrete. Very localised repairs
may not justify the use of stainless steel, as adjacent carbon steel would still be vulnerable.
Further guidance on the use of stainless steel reinforcement is provided in CS TR51 [Ref 51.I].

Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for concreting of
structural concrete

The nominal cover to reinforcement is defined in equation I.1 .

Equation I.1 Nominal cover to reinforcement
cnom = cmin + ∆c
where:
cnom
nominal cover to reinforcement, as illustrated in Figure 4 of BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N]and
Figure 1 of BS EN 13369 [Ref 10.N]
cmin
minimum cover to reinforcement, taken as the highest value between the minimum cover
required for durability to BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], the minimum cover required for the
transmission of bond forces to BS EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 30.N] and the minimum cover
required for fire resistance to BS EN 1992-1-2 [Ref 31.N]
∆c
tolerance to accommodate fixing precision, defined in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]as an
allowance for deviation
BS EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 30.N] and BS EN 1992-2 [Ref 28.N] require the minimum cover to be increased
by 5 mm for uneven surfaces, e.g. when exposed aggregates are specified for the structural element
and/or its part; by 5 mm for bare concrete decks of road bridges without waterproofing or surfacing; and
by 10 mm where a concrete surface is subject to abrasion caused by ice or solid transportation in
running water.
Where stainless steel reinforcement is specified for a structural concrete element and/or its part, and
with the exception of stainless steel reinforcement bridging joints or penetrating the concrete surface
such as dowel bars or holding down bolts, the minimum cover required for durability can be taken as no
more than 30 mm irrespective of the concrete composition and exposure class.
With reference to the notation for the geometric tolerances in BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N]and BS EN
13369 [Ref 10.N], the tolerance ∆c in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] is equivalent to |∆cmin us| , i.e. the
permitted minus-deviation from the nominal cover. ∆cmin us is equivalent to the term ∆cdev given in BS
EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 30.N] and BS EN 1992-2 [Ref 28.N].
In BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] it is advised that ∆c typically lies within the range 5 mm to 15 mm. For
highway structures, the tolerance ∆c for the cover to steel reinforcement and prestressing tendons in
cast in-situ and precast concrete elements shall be:
1)
10 mm, for cast in-situ concrete elements cast against formwork with height of cross section more
than 150 mm
2)
5 mm, for cast in-situ concrete elements cast against formwork with height of cross section equal
or less than 150 mm
3)
In accordance with Table 4 of BS EN 13369 [Ref 10.N], for precast concrete elements
4)
In accordance with Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], for cast-in situ concrete elements in
contact with prepared ground or directly in contact with soil.
The permitted plus-deviation ∆cplus , as illustrated in Figure 4 of BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N]and Figure 1
of BS EN 13369 [Ref 10.N], shall be:
1)
In accordance with Tables I.1 and I.2, for the cover to steel reinforcement in cast in-situ concrete
elements
2)
In accordance with Table 4 of BS EN 13369 [Ref 10.N], for the cover to steel reinforcement and
prestressing tendons in precast concrete elements

Further advice for the cover to reinforcement at permanent soffit formwork is given in CD 359 [Ref

Tables A.4 and A.5 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], dealing with XC, XD and XS exposures where
reinforcement corrosion is the deterioration mechanism, give a range of options for limiting values for
the composition and properties of concrete, related to the cover to reinforcement. The
recommendations given to resist freezing and thawing and chemical attack are such that, when
combined with the XC, XD and XS forms of attack, the cover to reinforcement does not need to be
increased, unless a sacrificial layer has been selected to resist chemical attack, in which case the cover
shall be increased by the layer thickness. However, where freeze-thaw resistant aggregates are
required, the concrete mix composition may be affected and this may, in turn, affect the range of options
for limiting values for the composition and properties of concrete related to the cover to reinforcement.
I3.1.2
Additonal design requirements, advice or notes for 'Applicable exposure classes'
Environmental and ground conditions are classified as exposure classes in Tables A.1 and A.2 of BS
8500-1 [Ref 12.N]. Table A.1 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]gives a non-exhaustive list of examples applicable
in the United Kingdom. All relevant exposure classes pertaining to the structural element and/or its part
shall be identified and provided. There may be specific structural elements where the exposure does
not readily fit the descriptions given in the exposure classes; in such situations, designers should use
engineering judgement. Particular attention should be given to culverts, subways, large horizontal
exposed surfaces, exposed/cantilever deck edges and retaining walls.
For the underside of cantilever deck edges of road over road bridges (where the soffit may be affected
by salt spray from the road carried by the bridge as well as from the road crossed) and for deck edge
beams exposure class XD3 shall be specified, in line with the recommendations in Table A.1 of BS

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
8500-1 [Ref 12.N]for bridge parapet edge beams and bridge parapets. For concrete below an effective
bridge deck waterproofing exposure class XC3 should be specified.
Where chemical attack of concrete buried/partially buried in ground needs to be considered for the
structural element and/or its part, the aggressive chemical environment for concrete (ACEC) exposure
class (ACEC-class) is derived from Table A.2 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], then converted into a design
chemical (DC) class through Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]. Further guidance on the selection of
the ACEC-class is provided in BRE SD-1 [Ref 84.I].
For the selected intended working life and specified nominal cover to reinforcement of a structural
element and/or its part, Tables A.4 to A.7 and A.9 to A.13 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] give the limiting
values for the composition and properties of concrete, when using a particular maximum size of
aggregate, to provide the required durability for each identified exposure class. For exposure classes
relating to the risk of corrosion of reinforcement induced by carbonation (XC classes) and by chlorides,
either from sea water (XS classes) or sources other than sea water such as de-icing salts (XD classes),
these limiting values are given in Tables A.4 and A.5 for intended working lives of at least 50 years and
at least 100 years respectively. For exposure classes relating to the environmental action of freezing
and thawing (XF classes), the given limiting values in Table A.9 work for both intended working lives of
at least 50 and at least 100 years. For highway structures with a design life of 120 years, the use of
concrete complying with the requirements of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] for an intended working life of at
least 100 years is classed as equivalent. However, there is a higher degree of uncertainty with the
recommendations for an intended working life of at least 100 years in the chloride (XD) and sea water
(XS) environment than that associated with the 50 years recommendations, and selective use of
stainless steel reinforcement or other techniques may be beneficial. Further guidance is given in CS
TR61 [Ref 37.I].
Having identified the limiting values for concrete composition and properties relevant to all identified
exposure classes pertaining to the particular structural element and/or its part (e.g. for all its concrete
faces), these values shall then be compared and the most onerous values selected and specified. The
values selected should be the highest strength class, the lowest maximum w/c ratio, the highest
minimum cement/combination content and cement/combination types which suit all the identified
exposure classes. The final limiting values shall be selected ensuring that a viable designed concrete is
achieved for the structural element and/or its part; this is particularly relevant where diverse exposure
conditions apply to a structural element and/or its part, e.g. an abutment or part of it subject to chlorides
on the front exposed face and with the rear face buried in highly aggressive ground conditions. For
concrete buried/partially buried in ground, it is advisable to start with the selection of the DC-class;
where the limiting values given in Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] cannot meet the requirements
dictated by the other exposure classes specified, another DC-class should be selected that meets
those requirements.
I3.1.3
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'DC-class'
The limiting values for some of the exposure classes for chemical attack and some of the test methods
in BS EN 206 [Ref 11.N] vary from current United Kingdom practice. The recommendations in BS
8500-1 [Ref 12.N]and BS 8500-2 [Ref 13.N] to protect concrete buried/partially buried in ground
exposed to sulfates are based on BRE SD-1 [Ref 84.I], that covers a wider range of environmental
actions than BS EN 206 [Ref 11.N] and includes mobile ground water, acids and brownfield sites. The
recommendations give precautions to minimise the risk of the occurrence of the thaumasite form of
sulfate attack (TSA) as well as the conventional ettringite form of sulfate attack. The risk of concrete
deterioration due to sulfate attack, including TSA, is worse where clays or other sulfide bearing
materials have been excavated, reworked and replaced adjacent to concrete buried/partially buried in
ground. The rapid oxidation of sulfides in disturbed ground, particularly pyrites, results in enhanced
levels of sulfates in the soil and groundwater.
The precautions to minimise the risk of concrete deterioration from chemical attack take the form of
designed concrete options, with additional protective measures (APMs) as necessary. The designed
concrete options are dependent upon a parameter to be specified termed the design chemical (DC)
class.
The DC-class is derived as follows:

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
1)
From the ground investigation data, and with reference to Table A.2 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], the
sulfate and magnesium ion concentration in the soil or groundwater, together with the 'potential'
sulfate that can arise from oxidation of sulfides, are used to derive the design sulfate class
(DS-class). Account is then taken of the type of site (natural soil or brownfield), the mobility of the
groundwater (static or mobile) and its pH. This leads to the aggressive chemical environment for
concrete (ACEC-class)
2)
The ACEC-class is then taken together with the thickness of the concrete section, the hydraulic
gradient due to groundwater (difference in hydrostatic head in metres divided by section
thickness in metres) and the intended working life of the structure (at least 50 or at least 100
years). This leads, in accordance with Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], to the DC-class and
the options for the APMs, which are listed in Table A.11 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N].
The selection of APM 1 (enhanced concrete quality) and/or the situations covered in the footnotes to
Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] can lead to a change in DC-class, and it is the final DC-class that
shall be specified.
For each DC-class, the limiting values for concrete composition and properties are given in Table A.12
of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N]. Further guidance is given in BRE SD-1 [Ref 84.I].
I3.1.4
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Additional protective measures'
Additional protective measures (APMs) are required for many of the DC-classes, and depending on the
DC-class the APM to be provided is, in accordance with Table A.10 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N], either a
prescribed one or a choice amongst some of the APMs listed in Table A.11 of BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N].
Guidance on the selection of APMs, which include the provision of controlled permeability formwork,
surface protection, sacrificial layer and addressing site drainage, is given in BRE SD-1 [Ref 84.I].
Specific guidance on controlled permeability formwork is provided in CBDG CPS 10 [Ref 30.I].
When surface protection is specified as an APM, the design outputs should require that it:
1)
provides an impermeable barrier
2)
is resistant to sulfates and other deleterious chemicals
3)
has a neutral effect on the concrete substrate
4)
is resistant to envisaged mechanical damage
5)
is easy to apply correctly
6)
has long term durability; and
7)
is cost effective.
Guidance and options for surface protection of concrete, which include coatings and water resisting
barriers, are given in BS EN 1504-2 [Ref 68.I] and BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 67.I].
A sacrificial layer can be achieved by providing an additional sacrificial thickness of cover concrete
integral with parent concrete, or by constructing a separate layer of concrete. When a sacrificial layer is
specified as an APM, the design outputs shall require that the quality of such a layer is at least equal to
that of the inner concrete; a 50 mm thickness is standard for a sacrificial layer. When a sacrificial layer
is specified as an APM, the design of the concrete element would need to be reappraised to reflect the
additional concrete, and the thickness of the sacrificial layer shall be ignored for the purpose of crack
width calculations. A sacrificial layer shall not be specified if the outer concrete layer has a structural
function, such as providing friction resistance.
Addressing site drainage should be selected as an APM when it is particularly important for aggressive
groundwater to be intercepted before coming into contact with concrete buried/partially buried in
ground, and for backfill to structures to be drained. For example, addressing site drainage should be
selected as an APM in cases of large excavations to deep foundations which can create sumps around
buried concrete, with the objective of providing drainage to the backfilled excavation and to prevent
groundwater entering from the surrounding area.
408
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
I3.1.5
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Required type of cement (or equivalent
combination)'
The types of cement/combination permitted in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] and BS 8500-2 [Ref 13.N] include
a wide range of supplementary cementitious materials, which can be combined with Portland cement to
form binary or ternary blends. Portland cement, ground granulated blast-furnace slag (GGBS), fly ash,
natural pozzolana, natural calcined pozzolana, limestone, silica fume are all covered, within certain
permitted relative proportion limits.
The choice of type of cement/combination is influenced by many parameters, such as the required
strength of the concrete and the required durability, the latter influenced by the selection of all
applicable exposure classes relevant to a structural element and/or its part. Therefore, the choice of a
specific type of cement/combination, associated to cement type-specific limitations on cement content
and water/cement ratio, is dictated by both strength and durability requirements, and not all the
permitted cements are therefore specifiable in all cases - this is reflected in the way the the specification
of designed concrete in BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] and BS 8500-2 [Ref 13.N] is set out. Further guidance on
the selection of the type of cement/combination is provided in TCC/03/66 [Ref 56.I].
As clinker is the principal constituent material controlling the embodied carbon emissions of concrete,
to minimise the carbon emissions the amount of Portland cement content should be limited as much as
possible when selecting a cement/combination type. This shall however be done once strength,
durability, functional design, construction practice requirements and ultimate fitness for purpose have
been considered and met. For example, the use of blended cements containing SCMs such as fly ash
and GGBS may increase concrete durability and resistance to both chloride ingress and sulfate attack,
but particular attention should be paid to possible delayed strength development and curing; when
rapid strength gain is important for practical purposes, it may not be possible to specify a concrete mix
with slower strength gain but whose Portland cement content is lower. Further guidance on specifying
sustainable concrete is provided in TCC/05/24 [Ref 85.I].
BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] uses the concept of combined performance categories to group different types of
cements/combinations based on their performance; these are defined in Table A.6 of BS 8500-1 [Ref
12.N]. It is therefore possible, at the design stage, to specify specific cement/combination types or to
specify a combined performance category, which is a group of cement/combination types of equivalent
performance.
Specifying a combined performance category rather than a specific type of cement/combination has the
advantage of not restricting the provision, at the construction stage, of a specific cement/combination
type that may not be available in stock from local concrete plants, and therefore permitting the selection
of an alternative of equivalent strength and durability performance. However, there may be specific
reasons dictating the selection of a specific cement/combination type, including requirements for
special cements as part of the specification; furthermore, not specifying a specific type of
cement/combination may lead to the concrete selected further down the line not being the lowest
carbon emitting one in the group.
CEM III/B or CIIIB are typically used for special applications such as when sulfate resistance of buried
concrete is required, to help minimise the risk of thermal cracking and where high resistance to chloride
ingress is required. However, they shall not be used in the surfaces of exposed concrete where there is
a risk of surface scaling under conditions of freezing and thawing.
I3.2
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for curing of concrete for structural
concrete
I3.2.1
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Additional special curing requirements'
Where structural elements are of considerable depth or bulk (for example where the sectional thickness
exceeds 600 mm), have a high proportion of cement (for example in excess of 400 kg/m3) or are
subject to accelerated curing, any specific curing method shall be specified in detail in the form of
additional special curing requirements, and low heat common cement or very low heat special cement
may be specified. This is because the higher the rate of development of strength in concrete (and
hence of heat of hydration of the cement), which is exacerbated if the temperature of the concrete is
409
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CD 350 Version 1.0.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 411 of 417
======================================================================

Appendix I. Additional design requirements, advice and no...
raised, the greater the need to prevent excessive differences in temperature within the concrete and
too rapid a loss of moisture from the surface, to minimise distortion and cracking.
The layout of reinforcement and the boundary conditions of a reinforced concrete element can affect
the restraint to the strains and trigger distortion and cracking due to early-age thermal effects, as
explained in CIRIA C766 [Ref 29.I]; the design outputs should include any special curing requirement
aimed at controlling early-age thermal cracking for the structural element and/or its part.
In cases where the construction stages are such that formwork may need to be removed before curing
is completed, the design outputs shall require that the curing environment associated to specified
curing class is maintained via other means until curing is completed.
Where it is envisaged that curing of the structural element and/or its part is to be undertaken in cold
weather (below 5°C), to prevent the formation of efflorescence the design outputs shall require that the
atmosphere adjacent to the surface of the concrete is maintained at a constant relative humidity
approaching 100% until removal of formwork.
When the structural element and/or its part subject to curing is to receive a waterproofing system, the
design outputs shall require that curing liquids, compounds or membranes are shown to be completely
removable by natural or mechanical means before being allowed to be used as a curing measure; in
particular, curing liquids can take a long time to disintegrate and may affect the appearance of
permanently visible surfaces as well as the bond of any waterproofing layer.
Texture, colour and durability of the concrete surface can be affected by curing. When a special surface
finish is specified for the structural element and/or its part, it may be necessary to specify specific
curing methods, including limits on the time of removal of formwork; in such cases, the design outputs
should require that structural elements and/or their parts that are intended to have the same surface
finish are subject to the same curing.
I3.3
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for formed surface finish for
structural concrete
I3.3.1
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Formed surface finish class'
The type and class of surface finish required depend on the nature of the element and its final position
in the structure, and may vary from face to face for the same element. Formed surface finish is
specified by reference to five standard classes (F1 to F5), but other non-standard formed finish whose
description falls outside of those for F1 to F5 can also be specified. The description of the standard
classes (F1 to F5) of formed surface finish and advice on their selection are provided in Table I.3.
Further advice and guidance is provided in CD 351 [Ref 102.N], CS TR52 [Ref 63.I], CS 170 [Ref
94.I]and CS OS8 [Ref 27.I].

Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for unformed surface finish for

I3.4.1
Additional design requirements, advice or notes for 'Unformed surface finish class'
The type and class of surface finish required depend on the nature of the element and its final position

classes (U1 to U5) of unformed surface finish and advice on their selection are provided in Table I.4.
Further advice and guidance is provided in CD 351 [Ref 102.N], CS TR52 [Ref 63.I], CS 170 [Ref

Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for geometrical
tolerances for structural concrete
I4.1
Design requirements, advice and notes for design outputs for specific geometrical tolerances
for structural concrete construction
I4.1.1
Additional design requirement, advice or notes for 'Specific requirements for stricter or
additional geometrical tolerances'
For aesthetic, durability and fitting purposes or for special structures, in Table C.62 of Appendix C it is
permitted to specify specific geometrical tolerances stricter than, or additional to, the default values.
The default values of the geometrical tolerances for structural concrete against which specific
requirements for stricter or additional geometrical tolerances can be specified are given:
1)
In the part of this Appendix containing additional design requirements, advice or notes for
'Nominal cover to reinforcement', for the geometrical tolerances related to the nominal cover to
reinforcement
2)
In the requirements below, for all other types of geometrical tolerances.
Geometrical tolerances for structural concrete shall be compliant with Section 10 of BS EN 13670 [Ref
45.N]as amended and complemented by this document. The provisions and geometrical tolerances in
10.2 to 10.8 and Annex G of BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N], which were derived specifically for building
structures, shall not apply to structural concrete for highway structures. The box principle, as defined in
BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N], shall not apply to structural concrete for highway structures.
The permitted deviations for the length of cross-sectional dimensions of in-situ concrete beams, slabs
and columns, as illustrated in Figure 4 of BS EN 13670 [Ref 45.N], shall be in accordance with Table I.5.
